1.1	CONTRASTIVE LINGUISTICS
The section 00 contrastive linguistics includes an overview of the origin,
concepts, objectives procedures 20٥ applications of CL.
Contrastive linguists 15 concerned with comparing 0 ٧0ا[ more languages
for the purpose of identifying similarities and differences that hold between
the languages compared as well as potential areas of learning problems. This
٤٧00 of synchronic contrastive studies should be distinguished fiom those
diachronic comparative studies that are part of the field 0٤ historical
linguistics which 15 concerned with comparing languages ٤0٣ the purpose of
reconstructing proto-languages.
11 interest in CL originated 10 the 5ل during 1 Second World War, when
20 urgent need arose 10٤ ٣19 teaching of foreign languages 111 21 economical
way. The pioneering work 0٤ linguists such 28 Fries (1945) 210 1200 (1957)
paved ٤109 way ٤03 م series ofpedagogically motivated contrastive projects
that involved 2 comparison 0لم English and other world languages such as
Spanish, German, Italian, 101102009 Polish, Swedish, Danish and Serbo-
Croatian.

The early proponents of contrastive linguistics (Fries and Lado) make three
major claims. First, they claim that differences between the languages
compared (11 18 1 learner's native language, and 12 8 the target language)
cause difficulties ٤0 111 learner of 12. Second, interference (negative
transfer) from 1 10 12 is a major source ofleaming difficulties or errors.
Third, 11 contrastivist can predict areas ofleaming difficulties on the basis
of contrasts that ع]ه identified 1 0 process of comparing the two
languages. 18ن strong version of 10ع contrastive analysis hypothesis has
created ه plethora of criticisms 214 doubts about the value of contrastive
studies.(For a review ofthe criticisms and replies generated in the literature
on CL, see James 1971.)
The concept 0٤ transfer constitutes 10ه psychological foundation 00 which
CL 15 based. The transfer hypothesis 18 1ه essential component 0٤ the
psychology 0٤ learning It claims 108ا the learning oftaskl influences or
interferes with the learning of task 2. Learners of 12 tend to transfer some
features 011 ٤1٥ م system 10 1 12 system. 18ن type 0 1 10 12 transfer 18
called "proactive", while 12 10 11 transfer is called "retroactive".
Contrastivists are interested in proactive transfer.
Since transfer is و central issue CL, we need to discuss it in more detail.
Transfer 00101 112 ٤0 1ر may be either positive 0٤ negative. Positive transfer
occurs when 11 and 12 have similar systems or features. This transfer
induces facilitation in learning 011 the other hand, negative transfer
(interference) occurs when LI and 12 have different systems 0 features. The
early contrastivists claimed that interference 18 a major source ofleaming
difficulty. Much 0٤ the controversy about 100 value of CL has centered
around the strong claim regarding the powerfill role 910 11 ا learning 12
As stated earlier, transfer 40100 11 has been accorded a significant role in
language learning, especially during the 408"50 5ا and 6O's. Two types of
studies were conducted 0 the phenomena of transfer. The first focussed on
11 00000111011 0٤ learning problems (errors) that could 0ه attributed 10
interference from the LI, e.g., Mukattash (1986), and others. The findings
of most 0 these studies indicated that about one third of errors committed
012 ل learners with different 11س backgrounds were attributed ٤0 interference,
(see Dulay, Burt and Krashen 1982 for 2 summay of research findings
regarding 111٥. incidence of interlingual (interference) vs developmental

errors.) The second 100٥ 0 study, on the Other hand, focussed 01 the
language behaviours 0٤ 12 learners (interlanguages) for the purpose 0٤
experimentally testing the predictions of contrastive studies, e g., 1311٥1
(1964), Selinker (1966) and Nemser(1971). A major finding of these early
studies ٧28 that transfer is a "selection process" rather than an automatic,
inevitable either-or process 1012 learning.
During the late 6O'S and early 7O'S there occurred a shift of focus away from
classical 0٨ towards error analysis (12٨) and interlanguage (IL) studies. This
shift coincided with 2 shift 111 the two fields oflinguistics and psychology.
There was a shift 1000 structural linguistics 0ا transformational-generative
grammar and one from behaviorist to cognitive theory 0ل learning.
The work مم Corder (1967) and Richards (1971) has been instrumental م the
advances made in 119 error analysis movement- Error analysis has been used
as 2 technique 11 second language acquisition research- Within 119 EA
framework, 10ع learner acquires the 12 0٧ testing 10009505 about the
systems 0٤ 11012 ه. Moreover, intralingual and developmental factors and
processes are 28 important in 12 learning 25 interlingual ones (interference).
These processes explain 1 causes of learner 21105. 10 example, Richards
(1971) identified four processes: overgeneralizaton, incomplete application
of rules, ignorance 0٤ rule restriction, and false concepts hypothesized.
*Earlier, Selinker (1972) identified five processes vital for the description of
the learners' ILs: overgeneralization, language transfer, transfer of training,
second language learning strategies and second language communication
strategies.
In sum, CA, EA and IL studies constitute three interrelated phases of the
research 01 12 learning.

The عناهسه variety used in this book :و MSA مممهييعم is occasionally made

14 LINGUISTIC MODEL AND COMPARABILITY
009 of 109 prerequisites for cartying out contrastive studies is that the two
contrasted languages should be described within the framework of the same
linguistic theoty. In this study, 11٥ grammatical systems of English and
Arabic are described within 2 structuralist linguistic model. Two factors have
dictated the selection 0٤ this model: theoretical and pedagogical The
theoretical factor concerns the fact that English 125 been well described
within the various linguistic models, e.g., structuralist, transformationalist.
6
؛
tagmemic, case grammar, 1اه00نن1ل theory. In fact all these models have
used English 28 primary data for analysis. The grammatical analyses carried
out لأ ancient Arab grammarians were mainly descriptive and were based on
form, function 211ل usage. The analyses carried out by contemporary Arab
grammarians, 0٦ the other hand, are descriptive 28 well 28 transformational.
The pedagogical factor, 01 the 000٥٣ hand, relates 10 10ه ease with which the
contrastive 4412 that 4 structuralist model yields 021 00 transferred ٤0
pedagogical purposes and applications. Moreover, the linguistic background
and language competence of the non-native audiences 0٤ this book fall short
of the level required for the grasp ofthe linguistic facts as described by the
more complex, less pedagogically-oriented (though, perhaps, 1010
theoretically powerfol) models of linguistic analysis.
Each 0 11 two languages 15 described 111 its own right 00501٤9 the fact that
each has a linguistic tradition with different interests, approaches and
methods. This method 0 description, however, does not work when a
grammatical category' 15 non-existent in 009 of the 1٧0 languages. For
example, the modal auxiliary' verbs constitute a closed class in English but
are not generally described as such in Arabic. The meanings ofthe English
modals are rendered in 110٤ 2001جد only 1ل auxiliaries but 2180 by main verbs
and particles. Because of . this gap 11 the grammatical realizations of the
1000215 ٦٧ have decided 10 present the meanings ofeach English modal
followed by the translational equivalents in Arabic.
111 description of English grammar in this book draws heavily on Quirk et
al's (1985) comprehensivegrammar . The description of Arabic grammar, on
111 other hand, 15 based on various Arabic grammar books, 0011 ancient and
modem, as listed in the Bibliography.
In comparing the grammatical systems ofthe two languages we have taken
1110 account both congruence (formal correspondence) and semantic
equivalence, 111 other words comparability 15 based 011 two criteria: form
andfunction
111 order to compare 1٧0 languages that have different linguistic systems 211ل
traditions and that belong 10 two different language families, it was necessary
10 select a single, consistent model of linguistic analysis that would provide
a terminological framework that 15 most readily applicable ٤0 both languages.
1 adjudged the terminology and approach used by Quirk et al (1985) to be

suitable fol’ the description of the grammars ofthe contrasted languages. I
have, therefore, selected their system for the present study.

2.5.2	Rhythm
(a)	English Rhythm
Stress in English sentences tends to recur at regular intervals of time. This

(0) Arabic Rhythm

(i)	Morphology of English Tense and Aspect
The following description of the English tense and aspect is based on Quirk
et al (1985).
Tense 15 2 grammatical category realized by verb inflection. It is relative 10
the time 0100 utterance. Past tense refers to past time, i e., time prior 10 the
time of the utterance, while present tense refers to present time, time
simultaneous with the time 0م the utterance.

Morphologically, English has only two simple tenses: past and present. (٨
simple tense refers 10 2 verb that is unmarked 10[ aspect.) The past 15
inflectionally marked either by suffixation (walk - walked) or by vocalic
change ("ablaut") (see - saw).. The present, 10 contrast, 18 unmarked except
1 the 31121800 ل singular form (live-s). The copula BE has three present
tense 1011108 (am, 15و are) and two past tense forms (was, were).
Aspect, 011 the other hand, indicates "...the way in which the verb action is
regarded 0+ experienced with respect ٤0 time.” (Quirk et 21 1985:27) English
has 11٧0 aspects: perfective and progressive 11ع perfective indicates
"anterior time" 21ل portrays the action 28 being complete, whereas 111٥
progressive indicates "simultaneous time" and portrays the action as being in
progress 22 ا given 11110٥.
1ع perfective :و marked by the auxiliary HAVE plus the -ed/-en participle
0٤ the ٧10, e g., 1 have 522 10. The progressive, on the 0100* hand, 18
marked by 100 auxilia1-y 1318لا01 ث the -111ى participle form of the verb, e g.. He
15 writing 2 letter.
(ii> Morphology 0٤ Arabic 121182 410 Aspect
The ancient Arab grammarians divided verbs into three types on the basis of
morphological formation:
(1)	maadii ماضي 'past ["perfect"]':	kataba كتب 'He wrote’
(2)	mud arمضارع '؛ 'present ["imperfect"]'	yaktubu يكتب 'He writes'
(3)	'amr أمر 'imperative':	'uktub أكتب 'Write!'
The Arabic ٧80 1888 two tenses: maadii ماضي ‘perfect’ (suffixed
conjugation) and mudaari' مضارع ‘imperfect’ (prefixed conjugation). The
perfect refers to past time while 18 Imperfect can typically refer to present
or future time. 11ع٦ two tenses, together with particles, express the various
aspectual meanings. Perfect tense indicates perfective aspect and imperfect
٤9108 indicates imperfective aspect. Perfective aspect indicates a completed
action, e g., takallama تكلم 'he talked', while imperfective aspect indicates
noncompleted action, e.g., kaanayatakallamu كان يتكلم 'he was talking'.
The morphology of the Arabic tenses consists of inflectional affixes that

mark 111 verb for person (152 واnd, 3rd), number (singular, dual, plural), and

5.5	DERIVATIONAL MORPHOLOGY
٨8 was stated earlier, morphology deals with two areas of word structure:
inflection and word formation. This section presents the morphological
processes of derivation that exist in English and Arabic.
Derivation means the formation of new words. 115ن can 9 achieved through
such morphological processes as affixation, compounding and
abbreviations.
3.3.1	Affixation
Affixation 15 2 productive morphological process 10 both English and Arabic.

bound morphemes that areattached at the beginning ofa word, whereas the
latter are attached at the end 0٤ 2 word. In contrast, Arabic has not only

advertisement), exam(ination), lab(oratory), mimeograph).

(Massachusetts Institute of Technology) radar (radio detecting and
ranging), AWOL (Away without leave).

() Adjective Comparison
The comparison of English adjectives 15 realized 0٧ either adding the
comparative (-er) and superlative (-est) inflectional suffixes or using the
periphrastic 011105 more 2011003٤ ل. The choice 04٧900 11 inflectional and

the stem.
(i) Monosyllabic words: These normally take the inflected forms -er, -est
tall-taller-tallest small-smaller-smallest big-bigger-biggest


(Past participial adjectives are exceptions: more, most bored.)
(؛(؛ (نsyllabic words: Some adjectives take the inflected forms, e.g.,
happier, happiest, cleverer, cleverest. 50100 others take only more
and most, e.g., awake, worried, carefill, flequent, public, private.
5000 others take either the inflected forms 01 more and most, e.g.,
gentler, more gentle, narrower, more narrow: simpler, more simple
(111) Trisyllabic words. 1195 always take more and most only, .ع.,
beautiful, practical.
Participial adjectives take 19 periphrastic form (100012/11005 interesting.)
Some adjectives ا00 0ل have comparative 20ل superlative forms, e.g.,
awake, male, unique.
Some adjectives take irregular inflection, e.g , old-elder-eldest good,
better, best, bad, worse, worst, 11٤112, less, 1248٤.

CONTRAST
The following differences hold between English 21ل Arabic prepositions.
First, English prepositions may ع either simple or complex whereas Arabic
01005 are all simple. Second, unlike English, some Arabic prepositions consist
of a letter or sound, e.g., ,albaa' الباء, ’al-laam اللام, 'al-kaaf الكاف, ’a,-taa’
التاء, ’al-waaw الواو. Third, unlike English prepositions, Arabic 01005 govern
111 noun that follows them 1٦٦ 11٥ genitive case. Fourth, unlike Arabic
prepositions, English ones may ع stranded or deferred, creating a
discontinuous prepositional phrase. Fifth, unlike Arabic prepositions, English
prepositions and prepositional phrases may be pre-modified by an adverb.
Sixth, unlike Arabic prepositions, English 0105 may have multiple class
membership, e.g., adverbs, conjuncts, ٧05. Seventh, unlike Arabic, English
has 2 large number of phrasal verbs that consist 0٤ 2 verb plus 2 particle that
may be either 2 0120051101 0٣ 211 adverb. 10[ example, the verb GIVE
0010011105 with several prepositions, each expressing 2 different relationship:
• GIVE: away, for, forth, 0٤1و out, up.
Eighth, English allows coordination of prepositions, whereas Arabic doesn't.
For example, 1119 following English sentence 15 rendered differently 111 Arabic:
٠	I go by car ٤0 21٤0111 ل the University
'aShabu ’ila 1-ami'ati ٧ auudu 11111144 bi-s-sayyaarati
أذهب إلى الجامعة وأعودمنها بالسيارة
(Literally: 1 go 10 the University 21ل come 02ا from it by car.)
Ninth, there 15 00 one-to-one correspondence between the meanings
expressed 0٧ English and Arabic prepositions. This contrast explains
interference errors made by Arab EFL learners ٧10 rely heavily 01 literal 0٣
word-for-word translation [101٦ English. For example, the English adjective

afraid is followed by of whereas 110ع Arabic adjective xaa’if خائف is
followed لاط min من (from). 11015 explains the erroneous phrase *afraid
from. Moreover, 111 some contexts, an English preposition may have a zero
equivalent in Arabic. For example, the prepositions 00و at and in 11 the
following sentences are 00ا realized لاا a preposition when translated into

6.3	MODALITY
Verbs are either foil verbs(e g , play) or auxiliary verbs (e.g , can, be). The
latter verbs are either primary auxiliaries (do, have, be) 0[ modal auxiliaries
(can, 10115٤ etc.) Modality is "the manner in which the meaning ofa clause is
qualified 50 25 10 reflect 11٦ speaker's judgement 0٤ the likelihood of the
proposition 1٤ expresses being true". (Quirk et al, 1985:21 9)
Modalities are expressed not only by modal verbs but also by modal adverbs
(possibly, probably, etc), modal adjectives (possible, probable, 2٤.) 210
modal nouns (possibility, probability, etc ). Arabic, 11 contrast, does not
have a distinct class م٥ modal verbs ٢٢٧11 clear-cut distinctions. Modal
meanings are realised ل particles qad قد rubbamaa ربما phrases mina 1-
waaj ibi من الواجب and lexical 0208 yastatii' ويستطيع yanbagii ينبغي
215
6.3.1	Forma! properties 0٤ English 000اهل verbs
English modal verbs have a number 0٤ 10110021 properties that distinguish 10111
from ٤111 verbs.
(a)	Modal verbs, except for ought ٤0, are usually followed by the bare
infinitive.
٠	He موع swim.
٠	We must leave.
(b)	They can only be inflected for tense. 10[ example, 2 does 00٤ have the
fonn *canning, but 0095 have 2 past tense form couJd, further examples are.
may - might, will - would.
(c)	They cannot take 13 عrd person singular present tense -5 inflection,
except for have has ٤0.
(d)	They are immediately followed by "00٤" 100 negative clauses.
*He cannot swim.
(e)	They admit subject-operator inversion in interrogative clauses.
٠	11مه ٥ sing.	Can he sing?
(10 They 888 carry nuclear stress and occur :» elliptical sentences.
*Won't you try again?	Yes, I WILL

(g)	Although some modals have present and past tense 10110115, the latter are
00ا proper past tense forms.

Concluding Remarks
Having highlighted the contrasts between the English 200 Arabic passive, we
will discuss the linguistic difficulties that Arab 1ا1ت learners 20ل English-
Arabic translators 1نه encounter when they use 0[ translate the English
passive. ٨5 has been stated earlier, English has two passive constructions:
agentive and agentless. In contrast, Arabic only has agentless 02853٧25. This
split phenomenon 19208 one 10 expect Arab EFL students ٤0 have some
difficulty ٦٧11 11 English passive. However, experienced EFL teachers find
2 different reality. At the 19٧215 of reception and guided production, Arab
EFL learners have very little difficulty with 11 English 02551٧9. That 18و they
understand 10 syntactic and semantic correspondence between the active
and 110 passive and can also convert active sentences into the passive. But, at
1 ٥ production level, both 1• speech and 11 writing, they very rarely 3لاً
the passive.
Why should there be such a discrepancy :و linguistic performance? Two
reasons for this may be hazarded. First, the formation of the English passive
18 more 000001٥* than that 0٤ the Arabic passive ٤011 0٤the verb. The former
involves object NP prepos1g and subject NP postposing, introducing the
BE auxiliary, the past participle 101100 0٤ the active verb and by which is then
attached 10 110 agentive phrase. In contrast, the passive form of the Arabic
266
ى1 10ح٧ derived from the active by internal vowel change. In addition, the
subject of the 02551٧ ٧2100 which 18 the logical object, has 2 nominative case-
ending.
Secondly, the English passive 15 100019 "marked" than the Arabic passive since
11٥ former 1يه both agentive and agentless passives whereas the latter only
128 agentless ones. Thirdly, since 1 formation of the English passive is
complex and since learners worry 2 10٤ about making errors, they often resort
to learning strategies such 25 avoidance (both partial 200ل complete) 20ل
simplification. Several researchers have 121002100 00 this avoidance strategy.
Mukattash (1983) found that Jordanian ESL learners avoided the use of the
English passive when they were asked to translate Arabic passive sentences
into English. They transposed the Arabic passives into English actives. In a
similar vein, Kleinmann (1977) tried to elicit certain English structures from
Arab, Spanish and Portuguese ESL learners 11 an experiment that involved
picture cues. He found 0٤ه the 20م٨ ESL learners avoided the use of the
passive. 0الا of 60 possible responses requiring the use ofthe passive, the
learners only produced 38 responses, nine correct and 29 incorrect 008
The above discussion مم the difficulties encountered by Arab EFL learners in
the use 0٤ the English passive shows the role of proactive interference, i.e.,
00100 Arabic into English. Retroactive interference, however, 15 evidenced by
the difficulties encountered 1ل Arab student translators only when translating
English agentive passives into ٨21. Agentless passives, however, pose 100
difficulty 51000 Arabic 4095 10٤ 2110٧ 11ع agent 10 appear in the surface
structure of passive sentences.
When Arab student-translators come across an English agentive passive

CONTRAST
English and Arabic relative constructions differ in a number of ways. First,
the relative 0101011 01ا English 255111005 111 grammatical function 0119
relativized noun, whereas 11 Arabic 10٥ resumptive pronoun assumes this
function. Second, 1٦ English 11 relative pronoun appears whether the
antecedent is definite or indefinite. 11٦ contrast, 110 Arabic, the relative
010110 115 10ل used only if the antecedent 15 definite. Third, 100 English the
relative pronoun and the copula (81021 (ت be deleted, whereas 11 Arabic 115ن
278
15 not possible. Fourth, 111 English the relative pronoun can be optionally
deleted when 1٤ replaces an object NP. In contrast, Arabic does 10٤ allow the
deletion of the 1121٧ pronoun since it fractions as a tie between the main
clause and the relative clause. Fifth, English, unlike Arabic, allows
preposition fronting, 1.0 , placing the preposition before the relative pronoun.
• The city about which you spoke 15 Jerusalem.
,al-madiinatu llatii tahaddae-ta 'ahaa hiya ,-quds
the-city which you-spoke about it it Jerusalem
المدينة التي تحدثت عنها هي القدس
(11ع city which لا0لا spoke about 15 Jerusalem.)
5 1*1 11ن21]٨ و relative clauses require a resumptive pronoun that 15
Deferential with the object relative pronoun, while English ones 0ل not 10
other words, Arabic requires pronominal anaphora 11 relative clauses, while
English requires zero anaphora. Finally, English relative pronouns 510٧
animate inanimate distinctions (٧110 ٧5. which), while Arabic relative
pronouns show number, gender and, only in the dual, case distinctions.

Conjunctions are uninflected words that 110 words, phrases 0٣ clauses

(b) Adverbial Clauses

CONTRAST
The following differences hold between English and Arabic conjunction.
First, 1109 Arabic waaw الواو 'and' frequently appears at the beginning of both
sentences ل0ه paragraphs. 10 contrast, English and' rarely occurs at the
beginning of sentences 211ل never 100 ٤ه beginning of paragraphs. Second, the
disjunctive 'or' has 100 counterparts 11 Arabic 120 أم and 'aw أو The
former Arabic particle conveys exclusive disjunction, while the latter conveys
inclusive disjunction. Finally, English and only appears before 11 last
constituent in a compound noun phrase or sentence. In contrast, the Arabic
waaw الواو appears before each constituent 0٤ 2 compound noun phrase.
